<tr>
<th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#ddd"><span style="font-weight:normal;">  <span class="reflink plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:ComparePages&amp;rev1=458612013&amp;page2=Celecoxib">(verify)</a></span></span></th>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Celecoxib</b> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/COX-2_selective" title="COX-2 selective">COX-2 selective</a> <a href="/wiki/Nonsteroidal_anti-inflammatory_drug" title="Nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drug">nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drug</a> (NSAID). It is used to treat the pain and inflammation of <a href="/wiki/Osteoarthritis" title="Osteoarthritis">osteoarthritis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rheumatoid_arthritis" title="Rheumatoid arthritis">rheumatoid arthritis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ankylosing_spondylitis" title="Ankylosing spondylitis">ankylosing spondylitis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acute_pain" title="Acute pain">acute pain</a> in adults, painful <a href="/wiki/Menstruation" title="Menstruation">menstruation</a>, and juvenile rheumatoid arthritis in people two years or older.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drug_Label_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Drug_Label-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Side effects include a 37% increase in incidence of major vascular events, which include nonfatal <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">myocardial infarction</a>, nonfatal <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>, or death from a blood vessel-related cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coxib2013_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coxib2013-3">[3]</a></sup> Additionally, an 81% increase in incidence of upper gastrointestinal complications occurs, which include <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_perforation" title="Gastrointestinal perforation">perforations</a>, obstructions, or <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_bleeding" title="Gastrointestinal bleeding">gastrointestinal bleeding</a> as in all NSAIDs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coxib2013_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Coxib2013-3">[3]</a></sup> In July 2015 the FDA strengthened the warning that non-aspirin nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs) can cause heart attacks or strokes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FDA_July_2015_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FDA_July_2015-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is marketed by <a href="/wiki/Pfizer" title="Pfizer">Pfizer</a> under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brand_name" title="Brand name">brand names</a> Celebrex and Celebra for arthritis and Onsenal for polyps. Celecoxib is available by <a href="/wiki/Medical_prescription" title="Medical prescription">prescription</a> in capsule form. Despite the <a href="/wiki/American_Heart_Association" title="American Heart Association">American Heart Association</a> warning that people at high risk for heart disease should only use celecoxib if there are not other possible treatments<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHA2007_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-AHA2007-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-consumerreports_2012_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-consumerreports_2012-6">[6]</a></sup> and that celecoxib costs about five times more than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acetaminophen" title="Acetaminophen">acetaminophen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibuprofen" title="Ibuprofen">ibuprofen</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Naproxen" title="Naproxen">naproxen</a> — which are equally effective for pain relief, 11 million prescriptions of celebrex are filled each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-consumerreports_2012_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-consumerreports_2012-6">[6]</a></sup> Celebrex was one of Pfizer's "best-selling drugs, amounting to more than $2.5 billion in sales [by 2012], and was prescribed to 2.4 million" people in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_2012_June_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_2012_June-7">[7]</a></sup> By 2012, 33 million Americans had taken Celebrex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_2012_June_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_2012_June-7">[7]</a></sup> As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the cost for a typical month of medication in the United States is more than $200.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ric2015_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ric2015-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_uses">Medical uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Medical uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Celecoxib is used for <a href="/wiki/Osteoarthritis" title="Osteoarthritis">osteoarthritis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rheumatoid_arthritis" title="Rheumatoid arthritis">rheumatoid arthritis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acute_pain" title="Acute pain">acute pain</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musculoskeletal_pain" title="Musculoskeletal pain">musculoskeletal pain</a>, painful <a href="/wiki/Menstruation" title="Menstruation">menstruation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ankylosing_spondylitis" title="Ankylosing spondylitis">ankylosing spondylitis</a>, and to reduce the number of colon and rectal polyps in people with <a href="/wiki/Familial_adenomatous_polyposis" title="Familial adenomatous polyposis">familial adenomatous polyposis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHFS2015_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-AHFS2015-9">[9]</a></sup> It may also be used in children with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Juvenile_rheumatoid_arthritis" title="Juvenile rheumatoid arthritis">juvenile rheumatoid arthritis</a> who are older than two years of age and weigh more than 10 kg (22 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHFS2015_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-AHFS2015-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>For postoperative pain, it is more or less equal to <a href="/wiki/Ibuprofen" title="Ibuprofen">ibuprofen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> For pain relief, it is similar to <a href="/wiki/Paracetamol" title="Paracetamol">paracetamol</a> (acetaminophen).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yelland_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yelland-11">[11]</a></sup> And in osteoarthritis, acetaminophen is the first line treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OARSI2007_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-OARSI2007-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> In knee and hip osteoarthritis, acetaminophen may be ineffective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>It was originally intended to relieve pain while minimizing the gastrointestinal adverse effects seen with conventional NSAIDs. In practice, its primary use is in people who need regular and long-term pain relief; probably, no advantage exists to using celecoxib for short-term or acute pain relief over conventional NSAIDs, except in the situation where nonselective NSAIDs or aspirin cause cutaneous reactions (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urticaria" title="Urticaria">urticaria</a> or "hives").<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mental_illness">Mental illness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Mental illness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Tentative evidence indicates its use in treating a number of psychiatric disorders, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Major_depression" title="Major depression">major depression</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bipolar_disorder" title="Bipolar disorder">bipolar disorder</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">schizophrenia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Familial_adenomatous_polyposis">Familial adenomatous polyposis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Familial adenomatous polyposis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It has been used to reduce colon and rectal polyps in people with familial adenomatous polyposis, but it is not known if it decreases rates of <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHFS2014_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-AHFS2014-20">[20]</a></sup> so it is not a good choice for this reason.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHFS2014_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-AHFS2014-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Adverse_effects">Adverse effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Adverse effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>Cardiovascular events: NSAIDs are associated with an increased risk of serious (and potentially fatal) adverse cardiovascular thrombotic events, including myocardial infarction and stroke. Risk may be increased with duration of use or pre-existing cardiovascular risk factors or disease. Individual cardiovascular risk profiles should be evaluated prior to prescribing. New-onset <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">hypertension</a> or exacerbation of <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">hypertension</a> may occur (NSAIDs may impair response to thiazide or loop diuretics), and may contribute to cardiovascular events; monitor blood pressure and use with caution in patients with hypertension. May cause sodium and fluid retention, use with caution in patients with edema or heart failure. Long-term cardiovascular risk in children has not been evaluated. Use the lowest effective dose for the shortest duration of time, consistent with individual patient goals, to reduce risk of cardiovascular events; alternative therapies should be considered for patients at high risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></li>
<li>Gastrointestinal events: NSAIDs may increase risk of serious gastrointestinal (GI) ulceration, bleeding, and perforation (may be fatal). These events may occur at any time during therapy and without warning. Use caution with a history of GI disease (bleeding or ulcers), concurrent therapy with aspirin, anticoagulants and/or corticosteroids, smoking, use of alcohol, the elderly or debilitated patients. Use the lowest effective dose for the shortest duration of time, consistent with individual patient goals, to reduce risk of GI adverse events; alternate therapies should be considered for patients at high risk. When used concomitantly with ≤325 mg of aspirin, a substantial increase in the risk of gastrointestinal complications (e.g., ulcer) occurs; concomitant gastroprotective therapy (e.g., proton pump inhibitors) is recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drug_Label_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Drug_Label-2">[2]</a></sup></li>
<li>Hematologic effects: Anemia may occur; monitor hemoglobin or hematocrit in people on long-term treatment. Celecoxib does not usually affect <a href="/wiki/Prothrombin_time" title="Prothrombin time">prothrombin time</a>, <a href="/wiki/Partial_thromboplastin_time" title="Partial thromboplastin time">partial thromboplastin time</a> or <a href="/wiki/Platelet" title="Platelet">platelet</a> counts; it does not inhibit platelet aggregation at approved doses.</li>
</ul>
<p>People with prior history of ulcer disease or GI bleeding require special precaution. Moderate to severe liver impairment or GI toxicity can occur with or without warning symptoms in people treated with NSAIDs.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allergy">Allergy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Allergy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Celecoxib contains a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulfonamide_(chemistry)" title="Sulfonamide (chemistry)">sulfonamide</a> moiety and may cause allergic reactions in those allergic to other sulfonamide-containing drugs. This is in addition to the contraindication in people with severe allergies to other NSAIDs. However, it has a low (reportedly 4%) chance of inducing cutaneous reactions among persons who have a history of such reactions to aspirin or nonselective NSAIDs. NSAIDs may cause serious skin adverse events, including exfoliative dermatitis, Stevens-Johnson syndrome, and toxic epidermal necrolysis; events may occur without warning and in patients without prior known sulfa allergy. Use should be discontinued at first sign of rash (or any other hypersensitivity).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heart_attack_and_stroke">Heart attack and stroke</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Heart attack and stroke">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The coxibs (which includes celecoxib) increase the risk of major cardiovascular problems by about 37%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coxib2013_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Coxib2013-3">[3]</a></sup> Naproxen does not significantly increase the rate of major vascular events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coxib2013_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Coxib2013-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The COX-2 inhibitor <a href="/wiki/Rofecoxib" title="Rofecoxib">rofecoxib</a> (Vioxx) was removed from the market in 2004 due to its risk. Like all NSAIDs on the US market, celecoxib carries an FDA-mandated "black box warning" for cardiovascular and gastrointestinal risk. In February 2007, the <a href="/wiki/American_Heart_Association" title="American Heart Association">American Heart Association</a> warned that with respect to "patients with a prior history of or at high risk for cardiovascular disease... use of COX-2 inhibitors for pain relief should be limited to patients for whom there are no appropriate alternatives, and then, only in the lowest dose and for the shortest duration necessary."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHA2007_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-AHA2007-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2005, a study published in the <i><a href="/wiki/Annals_of_Internal_Medicine" title="Annals of Internal Medicine">Annals of Internal Medicine</a></i> found that cardiovascular effects of COX-2 inhibitors differ, depending on the drug.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15684203_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15684203-22">[22]</a></sup> Other COX-2-selective inhibitors, such as rofecoxib, have significantly higher myocardial infarction rates than celecoxib.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11509060_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11509060-23">[23]</a></sup> In April 2005, after an extensive review of data, the FDA concluded it was likely "that there is a 'class effect' for increased CV risk for all NSAIDs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jenkins-24">[24]</a></sup> In a 2006 <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">meta-analysis</a> of randomized control studies, the <a href="/wiki/Cerebrovascular_disease" title="Cerebrovascular disease">cerebrovascular events</a> associated with COX-2 inhibitors were examined, but no significant risks were found when compared to nonselective NSAIDs or placebos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17176361_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17176361-25">[25]</a></sup> In 2016, a randomized trial found that celecoxib has "noninferior" cardiovascular safety to ibuprofen or naproxen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NissenYeomans2016_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-NissenYeomans2016-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Drug_interactions">Drug interactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Drug interactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Celecoxib is predominantly metabolized by <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome" title="Cytochrome">cytochrome</a> P450 2C9. Caution must be exercised with concomitant use of 2C9 inhibitors, such as <a href="/wiki/Fluconazole" title="Fluconazole">fluconazole</a>, which can greatly elevate celecoxib serum levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drug_Label_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Drug_Label-2">[2]</a></sup> If used concomitantly with lithium, celecoxib increases lithium plasma levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drug_Label_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Drug_Label-2">[2]</a></sup> If used concomitantly with warfarin, celecoxib may result in increased risk of bleeding complications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drug_Label_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-Drug_Label-2">[2]</a></sup> The drug may increase the risk of kidney failure with <a href="/wiki/Angiotensin" title="Angiotensin">angiotensin</a>-converting enzyme-inhibitors, such as <a href="/wiki/Lisinopril" title="Lisinopril">lisinopril</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Diuretic" title="Diuretic">diuretics</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrochlorothiazide" title="Hydrochlorothiazide">hydrochlorothiazide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Label_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Label-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pregnancy">Pregnancy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Pregnancy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the US FDA's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pregnancy_Category" title="Pregnancy Category">pregnancy categories</a>, the drug is category C prior to 30 weeks gestation, and category D starting at 30 weeks gestation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Label_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Label-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanism_of_action">Mechanism of action</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Mechanism of action">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-inflammatory">Anti-inflammatory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Anti-inflammatory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A highly selective reversible inhibitor of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/COX-2" title="COX-2">COX-2</a> isoform of <a href="/wiki/Cyclooxygenase" title="Cyclooxygenase">cyclooxygenase</a>, celecoxib inhibits the transformation of arachidonic acid to prostaglandin precursors. Therefore, it has antipyretic, analgesic and anti-inflammatory properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drug_Label_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-Drug_Label-2">[2]</a></sup> Nonselective NSAIDs (such as aspirin, naproxen, and ibuprofen) inhibit both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/COX-1" title="COX-1">COX-1</a> and COX-2. Inhibition of COX-1 (which celecoxib does not inhibit at therapeutic concentrations) inhibits the production of prostaglandins and the production of thromboxane A2, a platelet activator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drug_Label_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-Drug_Label-2">[2]</a></sup> COX-1 is traditionally defined as a constitutively expressed "housekeeping" enzyme and plays a role in the protection of the gastrointestinal mucosa, kidney hemodynamics, and platelet thrombogenesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mathew_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mathew-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Katzung_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Katzung-29">[29]</a></sup> COX-2, on the contrary, is extensively expressed in cells involved in inflammation and is upregulated by bacterial lipopolysaccharides, cytokines, growth factors, and tumor promoters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mathew_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mathew-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shi_S_and_Koltz_U_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shi_S_and_Koltz_U-30">[30]</a></sup> Celecoxib is approximately 10-20 times more selective for COX-2 inhibition over COX-1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Katzung_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Katzung-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> It binds with its polar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulfonamide_(chemistry)" title="Sulfonamide (chemistry)">sulfonamide side chain</a> to a hydrophilic side pocket region close to the active COX-2 binding site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DiPiro.2C_Joseph_T._2008_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-DiPiro.2C_Joseph_T._2008-32">[32]</a></sup> In theory, this selectivity allows celecoxib and other COX-2 inhibitors to reduce <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a> (and pain) while minimizing gastrointestinal <a href="/wiki/Adverse_drug_reaction" title="Adverse drug reaction">adverse drug reactions</a> (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stomach_ulcer" title="Stomach ulcer">stomach ulcers</a>) that are common with nonselective NSAIDs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-cancer">Anti-cancer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Anti-cancer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For its use in reducing colon polyps, celecoxib affects genes and pathways involved in inflammation and malignant transformation in tumors, but not normal tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Celecoxib binds to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cadherin-11" title="Cadherin-11">Cadherin-11</a> (which may explain the reduction in cancer progression).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Assefnia2014_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Assefnia2014-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure-activity_relationship">Structure-activity relationship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Structure-activity relationship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="floatright"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Monosubstituted_1,5-diarylpyrazoles.jpg"><img alt="Monosubstituted 1,5-diarylpyrazoles.jpg" data-file-height="514" data-file-width="901" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Monosubstituted_1%2C5-diarylpyrazoles.jpg/400px-Monosubstituted_1%2C5-diarylpyrazoles.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Monosubstituted_1%2C5-diarylpyrazoles.jpg/600px-Monosubstituted_1%2C5-diarylpyrazoles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Monosubstituted_1%2C5-diarylpyrazoles.jpg/800px-Monosubstituted_1%2C5-diarylpyrazoles.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a></div>
<div class="floatright"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Enzyme_data_for_monosubstituted_5-aryl_analogs.jpg"><img alt="Enzyme data for monosubstituted 5-aryl analogs.jpg" data-file-height="866" data-file-width="1173" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Enzyme_data_for_monosubstituted_5-aryl_analogs.jpg/400px-Enzyme_data_for_monosubstituted_5-aryl_analogs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Enzyme_data_for_monosubstituted_5-aryl_analogs.jpg/600px-Enzyme_data_for_monosubstituted_5-aryl_analogs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Enzyme_data_for_monosubstituted_5-aryl_analogs.jpg/800px-Enzyme_data_for_monosubstituted_5-aryl_analogs.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a></div>
<div class="floatright"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Enzyme_data_for_4-substituted_analogs.jpg"><img alt="Enzyme data for 4-substituted analogs.jpg" data-file-height="504" data-file-width="938" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Enzyme_data_for_4-substituted_analogs.jpg/400px-Enzyme_data_for_4-substituted_analogs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Enzyme_data_for_4-substituted_analogs.jpg/600px-Enzyme_data_for_4-substituted_analogs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Enzyme_data_for_4-substituted_analogs.jpg/800px-Enzyme_data_for_4-substituted_analogs.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a></div>
<div class="floatright"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:In_vitro_cox-I_and_cox-II_enzyme_data_for_disubstituted_5-aryl_analogs.jpg"><img alt="In vitro cox-I and cox-II enzyme data for disubstituted 5-aryl analogs.jpg" data-file-height="422" data-file-width="981" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/In_vitro_cox-I_and_cox-II_enzyme_data_for_disubstituted_5-aryl_analogs.jpg/400px-In_vitro_cox-I_and_cox-II_enzyme_data_for_disubstituted_5-aryl_analogs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/In_vitro_cox-I_and_cox-II_enzyme_data_for_disubstituted_5-aryl_analogs.jpg/600px-In_vitro_cox-I_and_cox-II_enzyme_data_for_disubstituted_5-aryl_analogs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/In_vitro_cox-I_and_cox-II_enzyme_data_for_disubstituted_5-aryl_analogs.jpg/800px-In_vitro_cox-I_and_cox-II_enzyme_data_for_disubstituted_5-aryl_analogs.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a></div>
<p>The Searle research group found the two appropriately substituted aromatic rings must reside on adjacent positions about the central ring for adequate COX-2 inhibition. Various modifications can be made to the 1,5-diarylpyrazole moiety to deduce the structure-activity relationship of celecoxib.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Penning_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Penning-36">[36]</a></sup> A para-sulfamoylphenyl at position 1 of the pyrazole was found to have a higher potency for COX-2 selective inhibition than a para-methoxyphenyl (see structures 1 and 2, below). In addition, a 4-(methylsulfonyl)phenyl or 4-sulfamoylphenyl is known to be necessary for COX-2 inhibition. For instance, replacing either of these entities with a –SO<sub>2</sub>NHCH<sub>3</sub> substituent diminishes COX-2 inhibitory activity as noted with a very high inhibitory concentration-50 (see structures 3 - 5). At the 3-position of the pyrazole, a trifluoromethyl or difluoromethyl provides superior selectivity and potency compared to a fluoromethyl or methyl substitution (see structures 6 – 9).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Penning_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Penning-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Celecoxib is compound 22; the 4-sulfamoylphenyl on the 1-pyrazol substituent is required for COX-2 inhibition and the 4-methyl on the 5-pyrazol system has low steric hindrance to maximize potency, while the 3-trifluoromethyl group provides superior selectivity and potency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Penning_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Penning-36">[36]</a></sup> To explain the selectivity of celecoxib, it is necessary to analyze the free energy of binding difference between the drug molecule and COX-1 compared to COX-2 enzymes. The structural modifications highlight the importance of binding to residue 523 in the side binding pocket of the cyclooxygenase enzyme, which is an isoleucine in COX-1 and a valine in COX-2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Price-37">[37]</a></sup> This mutation appears to contribute to COX-2 selectivity by creating steric hindrance between the sulfonamide oxygen and the methyl group of Ile523 that effectively destabilizes the celecoxib-COX-1 complex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Price-37">[37]</a></sup> Thus, it is reasonable to expect COX-2-selective inhibitors to be more bulky than nonselective NSAIDs.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Discovery_and_development_of_cyclooxygenase_2_inhibitors" title="Discovery and development of cyclooxygenase 2 inhibitors">Discovery and development of cyclooxygenase 2 inhibitors</a></div>
<p>Two lawsuits arose over discovery of celecoxib. Daniel L. Simmons of <a href="/wiki/Brigham_Young_University" title="Brigham Young University">Brigham Young University</a> discovered the COX-2 enzyme in 1988,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScientistBYULitig_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-ScientistBYULitig-38">[38]</a></sup> and in 1991 BYU entered into a collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Monsanto" title="Monsanto">Monsanto</a> to develop drugs to inhibit it. Monsanto was later purchased by pharmaceutical company <a href="/wiki/Pfizer" title="Pfizer">Pfizer</a>, and in 2006 BYU sued Pfizer for breach of contract, claiming Pfizer did not properly pay contractual royalties back to BYU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deseret_News_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deseret_News-39">[39]</a></sup> A settlement was reached in April 2012 in which Pfizer agreed to pay $450 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Settlement_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Settlement-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Other important discoveries in COX-2 were made at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Rochester" title="University of Rochester">University of Rochester</a>, which patented the discoveries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> When the patent issued, the university sued Searle (later Pfizer) in a case called, <i>University of Rochester v. G.D. Searle &amp; Co.</i>, 358 F.3d 916 (Fed. Cir. 2004). The court ruled in favor of Searle in 2004, holding in essence that the university had claimed a method requiring, yet provided no written description of, a compound that could inhibit COX-2 and therefore the patent was invalid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hodgsonruss_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-hodgsonruss-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kayescholer_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-kayescholer-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="National Academy of Sciences">National Academy of Sciences</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Needleman" title="Philip Needleman">Philip Needleman</a>, who was vice president of <a href="/wiki/Monsanto" title="Monsanto">Monsanto</a> in 1989 and president of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G._D._Searle_%26_Company" title="G. D. Searle &amp; Company">Searle</a> in 1993<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASO_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASO-45">[45]</a></sup> oversaw research into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/COX-2" title="COX-2">COX-2</a> that led to the development of the anti-inflammatory drug celecoxib (Celebrex).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASO_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASO-45">[45]</a></sup> He became senior executive vice president and chief scientist of <a href="/wiki/Pharmacia" title="Pharmacia">Pharmacia</a> from 2000 to 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASO_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-NASO-45">[45]</a></sup> Celecoxib was discovered and<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> developed by G. D. Searle &amp; Company and was approved by the FDA on December 31, 1998.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> It was co-promoted by Monsanto Company (parent company of Searle) and Pfizer under the brand name Celebrex. Monsanto merged with <a href="/wiki/Pharmacia" title="Pharmacia">Pharmacia</a>, from which the Medical Research Division was acquired by Pfizer, giving Pfizer ownership of Celebrex. The drug was at the core of a major patent dispute that was resolved in Searle's favor (later Pfizer) in 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hodgsonruss_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-hodgsonruss-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kayescholer_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-kayescholer-44">[44]</a></sup> In <i>University of Rochester v. G.D. Searle &amp; Co.</i>, 358 F.3d 916 (Fed. Cir. 2004), the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Rochester" title="University of Rochester">University of Rochester</a> claimed that United States Pat. No. 6,048,850 (which claimed a method of inhibiting COX-2 in humans using a compound, without actually disclosing what that compound might be) covered drugs such as celecoxib. The court ruled in favor of Searle, holding in essence that the University had claimed a method requiring, yet provided no written description of, a compound that could inhibit COX-2 and therefore the patent was invalid.</p>
<p>After the withdrawal of rofecoxib from the market in September 2004, Celebrex enjoyed a robust increase in sales. However, the results of the APC trial in December of that year raised concerns that Celebrex might carry risks similar to those of rofecoxib, and Pfizer announced a moratorium on <a href="/wiki/Direct-to-consumer_advertising" title="Direct-to-consumer advertising">direct-to-consumer advertising</a> of Celebrex soon afterwards. After a significant drop, sales of Celebrex have recovered, and reached $2 billion in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHA2007_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-AHA2007-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pfizer resumed advertising Celebrex in magazines in 2006,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berenson_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berenson-48">[48]</a></sup> and resumed television advertising in April 2007 with an unorthodox, <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap">2<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span>-minute advertisement which extensively discussed the adverse effects of Celebrex in comparison with other anti-inflammatory drugs. The ad drew criticism from the consumer advocacy group <a href="/wiki/Public_Citizen" title="Public Citizen">Public Citizen</a>, which called the ad's comparisons misleading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saul_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saul-49">[49]</a></sup> Pfizer responded to Public Citizen's concerns with assurances that they are truthfully advertising the risk and benefits of Celebrex as set forth by the FDA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saul_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Saul-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>In late 2007, Pfizer released another US television ad for Celebrex, which also discussed celecoxib's adverse effects in comparison with those of other anti-inflammatory drugs.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fabricated_efficacy_studies">Fabricated efficacy studies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Fabricated efficacy studies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pfizer" title="Pfizer">Pfizer</a> and its partner, <a href="/wiki/Pharmacia" title="Pharmacia">Pharmacia</a> presented findings from their study that Celebrex was "better in protecting the stomach from serious complications than other drugs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_2012_June_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_2012_June-7">[7]</a></sup> This became Celebrex's main selling point. However, following federal investigations it was revealed that Pfizer and Pharmacia "only presented the results from the first six months of a year long study rather than the whole thing." These partial results were then published in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Journal_of_the_American_Medical_Association" title="The Journal of the American Medical Association">The Journal of the American Medical Association</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_2012_June_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_2012_June-7">[7]</a></sup> In 2001 <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> released the full results of the Pfizer and Pharmacia study which showed that they had withheld crucial data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_2012_June_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_2012_June-7">[7]</a></sup> By 2012 a federal judge unsealed "thousands of pages of internal documents and depositions" in a "long-running securities fraud case against Pfizer."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_2012_June_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_2012_June-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 11, 2009, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scott_S._Reuben" title="Scott S. Reuben">Scott S. Reuben</a>, former chief of acute pain at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baystate_Medical_Center" title="Baystate Medical Center">Baystate Medical Center</a>, Springfield, Massachusetts, revealed that the data for 21 studies he had authored for the efficacy of the drug (along with others such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vioxx" title="Vioxx">Vioxx</a>) had been fabricated. The analgesic effects of the drugs had been exaggerated. Reuben was also a former paid spokesperson for Pfizer. Although from 2002 to 2007 Pfizer underwrote much of Dr. Reuben's research and "many of his trials found that Celebrex and Lyrica, Pfizer drugs, were effective against postoperative pain," Pfizer was not aware of the fraudulent data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt0311_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt0311-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt0311_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt0311-50">[50]</a></sup> None of the retracted studies was submitted to either the US <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> or the European Union's regulatory agencies prior to the drug's approval. Although Pfizer issued a public statement declaring, "It is very disappointing to learn about Dr. Scott Reuben's alleged actions. When we decided to support Dr. Reuben's research, he worked for a credible academic medical center and appeared to be a reputable investigator",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJ-3-2009_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ-3-2009-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> the documents unsealed in 2012 revealed that by February 2000, Pharmacia employees had devised a strategy to present the findings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_2012_June_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_2012_June-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Availability">Availability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Availability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Pfizer markets celecoxib under the brand name Celebrex, and it is available as oral capsules containing 50, 100, 200 or 400 mg of celecoxib.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is legally available in many jurisdictions as a generic under several brand names.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> In the US, celecoxib was covered by three patents, two of which expired May 30, 2014, and one of which (US RE44048<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>) was due to expire December 2, 2015. On March 13, 2014, that patent was found to be invalid for <a href="/wiki/Double_patenting" title="Double patenting">double patenting</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Upon the patent expiry on May 30, 2014, FDA approved the first versions of celecoxib generic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancer_prevention">Cancer prevention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Cancer prevention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The role celecoxib might have in reducing the rates of certain cancers has been the subject of many studies. However, no current medical recommendation exists to use this drug for cancer reduction.</p>
<p>The use of celecoxib to reduce the risk of <a href="/wiki/Colorectal_cancer" title="Colorectal cancer">colorectal cancer</a> has been investigated, but neither celecoxib nor any other drug is indicated for this use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Small-scale clinical trials in very high-risk people (belonging to FAP families) showed celecoxib can prevent polyp growth. Hence, large-scale randomized clinical trials were undertaken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bertagnolli_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bertagnolli-59">[59]</a></sup> Results show a 33 to 45% polyp recurrence reduction in people treated with celecoxib each day. However, serious cardiovascular events were significantly more frequent in the celecoxib-treated groups. Aspirin shows a similar (and possibly larger) protective effect,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baron_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baron-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandler_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sandler-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bosetti_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bosetti-62">[62]</a></sup> has demonstrated cardioprotective effects and is significantly cheaper, but no head-to-head clinical trials have compared the two drugs.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancer_treatment">Cancer treatment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Cancer treatment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Different from cancer prevention, cancer treatment is focused on the therapy of tumors that have already formed and have established themselves inside the patient. Many studies are going on to determine whether celecoxib might be useful for this latter condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dannenberg_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dannenberg-63">[63]</a></sup> However, during molecular studies in the laboratory, it became apparent that celecoxib could interact with other intracellular components besides its most famous target, COX-2. The discovery of these additional targets has generated much controversy, and the initial assumption that celecoxib reduces tumor growth primarily by the inhibition of COX-2 became contentious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Certainly, the inhibition of COX-2 is paramount for the anti-inflammatory and analgesic function of celecoxib. However, whether inhibition of COX-2 also plays a dominant role in this drug's anticancer effects is unclear. For example, a recent study with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malignant_tumor" title="Malignant tumor">malignant tumor</a> cells showed celecoxib could inhibit the growth of these cells <i><a href="/wiki/In_vitro" title="In vitro">in vitro</a></i>, but COX-2 played no role in this outcome; even more strikingly, the anticancer effects of celecoxib were also obtained with the use of cancer cell types that do not even contain COX-2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chuang_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chuang-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Additional support for the idea that other targets besides COX-2 are important for celecoxib's anticancer effects has come from studies with chemically modified versions of celecoxib. Several dozen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analog_(chemistry)" title="Analog (chemistry)">analogs</a> of celecoxib were generated with small alterations in their <a href="/wiki/Chemical_structure" title="Chemical structure">chemical structures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhu_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-zhu-66">[66]</a></sup> Some of these analogs retained COX-2 inhibitory activity, whereas many others did not. However, when the ability of all these compounds to kill tumor cells in <a href="/wiki/Cell_culture" title="Cell culture">cell culture</a> was investigated, the antitumor potency did not at all depend on whether or not the respective compound could inhibit COX-2, showing the inhibition of COX-2 was not required for the anticancer effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhu_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-zhu-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sch.C3.B6nthal-analogs_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sch.C3.B6nthal-analogs-67">[67]</a></sup> One of these compounds, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=2,5-dimethyl-celecoxib&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="2,5-dimethyl-celecoxib (page does not exist)">2,5-dimethyl-celecoxib</a>, which entirely lacks the ability to inhibit COX-2, actually displayed stronger anticancer activity than celecoxib.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sch.C3.B6nthal-antitumor_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sch.C3.B6nthal-antitumor-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
